<div id="review_1058591892" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1058591892" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5010988-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391325880p2/5010988.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1058591892?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/5010988-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5010988-laura?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5010988-laura?shelf=mount-tbr-2018">mount-tbr-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5010988-laura?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1058591892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8752996960312702124">Essential reading - I don’t know how I didn’t come across this sooner in my education.<br/><br/>In 1959, John Howard Griffin decided to try to pass as a black man, to experience that life in America. He spent time in New Orleans under the supervision of a dermatologist, taking pills to change his melanin & laying under heat lamps. He got darker & darker, until one night he left his friend’s house, & passed into the city as a black man. He expected to experience racism & the challenges of segregation. But </span>
  <span id="freeText8752996960312702124" style="display:none">Essential reading - I don’t know how I didn’t come across this sooner in my education.<br /><br />In 1959, John Howard Griffin decided to try to pass as a black man, to experience that life in America. He spent time in New Orleans under the supervision of a dermatologist, taking pills to change his melanin &amp; laying under heat lamps. He got darker &amp; darker, until one night he left his friend’s house, &amp; passed into the city as a black man. He expected to experience racism &amp; the challenges of segregation. But he was demolished by how much hate was shown to him in the White Stare. He couldn’t have understood how difficult it was even to get to a bathroom as a black man unless he had lived it; wouldn’t have known how white men would talk to black men about sex unless he had hitched rides through Alabama; would never have felt the fear of walking down the street in a place where people hate you just for the color of your skin.<br /><br />Sadly, this book is still relevant today. The details have changed, but not the substance. And when you read his indictments of Christians &amp; patriots almost 60 years ago, it will sting to realize he could walk through America as a black man today &amp; come to the same conclusions. But that’s why it’s essential reading, especially for white people. <br /><br />Griffin is a role model for people wanting to make a difference. Wherever he found himself, he worked to break down the notion of The Other. As a teenager, he was in France during WWII. He smuggled Jewish children to safety, until he himself was put on a death list &amp; had to escape. He was later in the South Pacific, &amp; he served as an anthropologist as much as a soldier, making connections with the locals that led to victories over Japan. He was struck blind in that period, &amp; had to go home &amp; re-make his life as The Other, working to show his intrinsic humanity as a person with a disability. Upon spontaneous recovery of his sight a decade later, he dedicated his life to removing the stigma of Other-ness, working to show people how alike we all are. Quite frankly, he’s an American saint &amp; should be honored as such.<br /><br />It’s short. You have no reason not to read this. Go get your hands on it. Now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8752996960312702124" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1191352890" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1191352890" />
    <a title="Theresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7254722-theresa"><img alt="Theresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555094461p2/7254722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1191352890?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Theresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Theresa" href="/user/show/7254722-theresa">Theresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1191352890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7685390649756020559">4.5 stars<br/>I believe this book is an absolute must-read and truly well written and executed in all aspects.<br/>In this book, Griffin describes an experiment he undertook in the 1950s in America's deep South, where he turned his skin black and lived as a black man for a few months. The experience not only changed him, but opened many white people's eyes to the true plight of black America.<br/><br/>What I found most moving and revelatory about his account, is how quickly he began to identify with his "fellow" bl</span>
  <span id="freeText7685390649756020559" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br />I believe this book is an absolute must-read and truly well written and executed in all aspects.<br />In this book, Griffin describes an experiment he undertook in the 1950s in America's deep South, where he turned his skin black and lived as a black man for a few months. The experience not only changed him, but opened many white people's eyes to the true plight of black America.<br /><br />What I found most moving and revelatory about his account, is how quickly he began to identify with his "fellow" black people and started to internalize the horrible way in which he was being treated. I think it just goes to show that any human who is being consistently and unquestioningly treated as less than will eventually start to view himself in the same light.<br /><br />I also really liked how he wasn't trying to speak for black people, but instead worked to give them the voice they were refused at the time. White people had certain expectations of black people and black people had started to behave accordingly so as not to upset the whites, which then only served to perpetuate the stereotypes and the views of blacks as actually enjoying their status as second class citizens. This book seems to have at least served in part to break that cycle, to give white people a look behind a veneer they had erected for themselves.<br /><br />I think this book should still be required reading today and gives a lot of wonderful insights to people who aren't aware of the time of racial segregation in America. I'm glad I read this book and I urge everyone else to do the same.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7685390649756020559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2067083409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15824045285325679956"><br/>“If a white man became a Negro in the Deep<br/>South, what adjustments should he have to make? What is it like to experience discrimination based on skin color, something over which one has no control”? <br/><br/>At first, I was hesitant to read this book even though I had it for some time now. John Howard Griffin was a journalist who decided to “cross the line” . This happened at the end of the 1950’s into the 60’s were just John took interesting/extreme steps to become a “Negro” and that did bother me. <br/><br/>What </span>
  <span id="freeText15824045285325679956" style="display:none"><br />“If a white man became a Negro in the Deep<br />South, what adjustments should he have to make? What is it like to experience discrimination based on skin color, something over which one has no control”? <br /><br />At first, I was hesitant to read this book even though I had it for some time now. John Howard Griffin was a journalist who decided to “cross the line” . This happened at the end of the 1950’s into the 60’s were just John took interesting/extreme steps to become a “Negro” and that did bother me. <br /><br />What surprised me the most was the support he got from both blacks and whites. Of course it was not always peachy. The fact that John as a now “Black” man who could finally see what it was like for the “other side” was needed. It seemed as if his 6 week experience as a “Black” man was not as rough but he was seeing what was always right in face but yet he ignored, because even though he was not opposed to “Negros”, he never looked to see the lifestyle, judgement, and every trials a Negro man endured. <br /><br />John first ventured into Louisiana and did not stop there as he went deeper into the south where there was no justice at all for a “Negro” man. <br /><br /><br />I was able to read this without anger but instead, patience. Throughout, John Howard Griffin was not looking for solutions but understating the difference between blacks and whites, and he said best when he stated, “I concluded that, as in everything else, the atmosphere of a place is entirely different for Negro and white. The Negro sees and reacts differently not because he is Negro, but because he is suppressed. Fear dims even the sunlight”.<br /><br />In the end, Griffin could not handle it. The hatred became to much for him after 6 weeks of being a “Negro” man. So he scrubbed off his “blackness” and became white John Griffin again. <br /><br />This book was another step for John Griffin, as he continued to be an advocate, “that this country rid itself of the racism that prevented some citizens from living as fully functioning men and as a result dehumanized all men”.<br /><br />Note: When I googled John Howard Griffin as himself and as a “black” man, I truly do not know how he got away with it 🤔.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15824045285325679956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8171764139687894137">“Fear dims even the sunlight.” <br/>― John Howard Griffin, Black Like Me<br/><br/><br/>Reading this book made me think: I don't know anything. <br/><br/><br/><br/>It is so strange reading about times that I did not live through.   <span id="freeText8171764139687894137" style="display:none">“Fear dims even the sunlight.” <br />― John Howard Griffin, Black Like Me<br /><br /><br />Reading this book made me think: I don't know anything. <br /><br /><br /><br />It is so strange reading about times that I did not live through. And then reading about bold strong human beings who did live through them with a bravery one can only marvel at. Black like me is a work of Non Fiction and what a book it is. The writing is so without pretense and so absorbing. <br /><br />You really learn alot. And even if you think you know alot, reading word for tragic word what times were like back then. I remember after reading this book I said to a family member, "Now I get it". Of coarse I can't get it. Nobody can get everything who was not alive during that time period and did not experience what went on personally.<br /><br />We read all sorts of books for different reasons. This one should be mandatory reading. A superb and heart breaking book.</span>

<div id="review_269552134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269552134" />
    <a title="Kevin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4239462-kevin"><img alt="Kevin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1283468796p2/4239462.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269552134?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin" href="/user/show/4239462-kevin">Kevin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781609401085" href="/book/show/9918559-black-like-me">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269552134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9624205836136409914">It may be possible that more people have expended more words and energy on this book than any other that I've read and reviewed so far. That leaves me feeling that writing my own review is a daunting task since I'm competing with so many eminent scholars, historians, and professional writers.<br/>So I guess I have to just forget all of that and say, "Holy shit."<br/><br/>I find it impossible to believe that I wasn't asked to read this at some point during my schooling. I recognize that I grew up in the south a</span>
  <span id="freeText9624205836136409914" style="display:none">It may be possible that more people have expended more words and energy on this book than any other that I've read and reviewed so far. That leaves me feeling that writing my own review is a daunting task since I'm competing with so many eminent scholars, historians, and professional writers.<br />So I guess I have to just forget all of that and say, "Holy shit."<br /><br />I find it impossible to believe that I wasn't asked to read this at some point during my schooling. I recognize that I grew up in the south and that it's entirely possible that even in the 1980s and 1990s people in the Tennessee public school administration didn't really think this was appropriate for impressionable young minds. Or, you know, after you read a book like this you start thinking it's possible they didn't make us read it because, well, they're racist and this might give us the wrong idea. I don't know.<br /><br />All I know is that this was a mind-blowing book for me. At the start I thought his story and style was a bit overwrought; by the end I could hardly put it down, just dragged along on his journey and partly against my will.<br /><br />I don't know what to say. It's all still kind of churning inside. There were passages that made me hate humanity. There were passages that made me tear up. There were - and this is sad to say but I suppose it's one of Griffin's main purposes - passages that certainly made me question how I think and how I treat people. That's really distressing. I pride myself on being fair and treating everyone alike and to see so many people in the book saying the same things I'm saying and then the reactions that caused and<br /><br />and you know what I can't really type all this out. It's a conversation that you and I should have over drinks sometime, okay? Long story short I'm mad that I didn't read this 20 years ago. I know I wouldn't have understood it as well then. Maybe I needed to have lived here in New Orleans for a while (as opposed to my startlingly Caucasian hometown) to even recognize why <br /><br />I'm losing track of words again.<br /><br />Here's the thing that kills me the most, and then I'll shut up: I was born in 1976. To me, the Civil Rights movement has always been "over" and segregation was a thing of the past. My parents raised me to not use offensive slurs and to not judge people by the color of their skin, so to me this has just always been the way things are.<br /><br />To read the horrific things that happened to Griffin and to see the year 1959 referenced over and over and realize that it was just a little over fifty years ago is killing me. I want this to be long dead history and yet there's an entirely possible chance that some of the 20-year-old hate-filled white men in the story are still alive. The story opens in New Orleans. They could be still alive and <i>be my neighbors</i>. I can't stand that. It makes my stomach hurt. That's how powerful Griffin's story was - of course I've known racist people, but it just seemed like one person's problem. "Oh, <i>that guy</i> is racist." To see how in Griffin's eye <i>everyone</i> with whom he interacted was so intolerably heartless and hateful (whether through ignorance or intention, guess it doesn't matter) was killing me. This book frustrated and infuriated me by showing me just how <i>near</i> this past is, and I want so badly to believe we're past that, but obviously we're not. It's a cheap way out, a passive dislike for a shitty situation, and I guess that upsets me too because I feel like I should have been doing more this whole time.<br /><br />The part that really kicked me in the ass, the passage that felt almost like a personal rebuke, was this:<br /><br /><i>"It gives him a view of the white man that the white man can never understand; for if the Negro is part of the black mass, the white is always the individual, and he will sincerely deny that he is "like that," he has always tried to be fair and kind to the Negro. Such men are offended to find Negroes suspicious of them, never realizing that the Negro cannot understand how - since as individuals they are decent and "good" to the colored - the whites as a group can still connive to arrange life so that it destroys the Negro's sense of personal value, degrades his human dignity, deadens the fibers of his being."</i><br /><br />I can't give true power to that statement without all the pages of heartbreak that come before it, so all I can really tell you to do is go read this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9624205836136409914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44284842" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2648036633630816084">I read this as a kid in Texas in the early '70s and found it absolutely riveting. I suspect the reviewers who are annoyed that Griffin is so admired are much younger. Society has changed so much in the interim, pre-multicultural life must seem comparable to the Jurassic Period. For a white man to "cross over" in the Deep South in 1959 was truly brave; remember, he didn't necessarily understand exactly how he should act with white people, which put him in danger. This in no way negates or minimiz</span>
  <span id="freeText2648036633630816084" style="display:none">I read this as a kid in Texas in the early '70s and found it absolutely riveting. I suspect the reviewers who are annoyed that Griffin is so admired are much younger. Society has changed so much in the interim, pre-multicultural life must seem comparable to the Jurassic Period. For a white man to "cross over" in the Deep South in 1959 was truly brave; remember, he didn't necessarily understand exactly how he should act with white people, which put him in danger. This in no way negates or minimizes the danger that black people lived with every day, but for Griffin to knowingly and willingly take it on was remarkable.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2648036633630816084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer16681127713634039496">The very audacity that a white person's voice is stronger and louder than the collective suffering of the underclass makes this a discomforting read. What annoys me most about this book is that there are many authentic 'Black' voices that have been silenced throughout the years, yet this poorly written book is one that is held up as a beacon of what repression, racism and discrimination feels like - a phenomenon that basically says that unless you are white and experience suffering, then your ac</span>
  <span id="freeText16681127713634039496" style="display:none">The very audacity that a white person's voice is stronger and louder than the collective suffering of the underclass makes this a discomforting read. What annoys me most about this book is that there are many authentic 'Black' voices that have been silenced throughout the years, yet this poorly written book is one that is held up as a beacon of what repression, racism and discrimination feels like - a phenomenon that basically says that unless you are white and experience suffering, then your account is somehow diminished or of lesser value. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uCiG7krBv1PE/MHM1V/byZ1KIv1083dd1cJQlHnvcSIFck0tVh/qYaYw1EqdhLqLrTJcKMHsLIZ6cdWl0lFOVw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Du Bois (1868–1963) played a key role in developing the strategy and program that dominated early 20th-century black protest in America. In this collection of essays, first published together in 1903, he eloquently affirms that it is beneath the dignity of a human being to beg for those rights that belong inherently to all mankind. He also charges that the strategy of accommodation to white supremacy advanced by Booker T. Washington, then the most influential black leader in America, would only serve to perpetuate black oppression.\nPublication of The Souls of Black Folk was a dramatic event that helped to polarize black leaders into two groups: the more conservative followers of Washington and the more radical supporters of aggressive protest. Its influence cannot be overstated.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_51937'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51937.Man_in_the_Mirror?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Man in the Mirror: John Howard Griffin and the Story of Black Like Me<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/29312.Robert_Bonazzi?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Robert Bonazzi<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.16 avg rating &mdash; 55 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1997\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer9155034194750438162\">In 1959 a white writer darkened his skin and passed for a time as a \"Negro\" in the Deep South. Few readers know of the extraordinary journey that led<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9155034194750438162\" style=\"display:none\">In 1959 a white writer darkened his skin and passed for a time as a &quot;Negro&quot; in the Deep South. John Howard Griffin was that writer, and his book Black Like Me swiftly became a national sensation. Few readers know of the extraordinary journey that led to Griffin\'s risky &quot;experiment&quot; - the culmination of a lifetime of risk, struggle, and achievement. A native of Texas, Griffin was a medical student who became involved in the rescue of Jews in occupied France; a U.S. serviceman among tribal peoples in the South Pacific, where he suffered an injury that left him blinded for a decade; a convert to Catholicism; and, finally, a novelist and writer. All these experiences fed Griffin\'s drive to understand what it means to be human, and how human beings can justify treating their fellows - of whatever race or physical description - as &quot;the intrinsic other.&quot; After describing this journey and analyzing the text of Black Like Me, Robert Bonazzi treats the dramatic aftermath of Griffin\'s experiment and life. Man in the Mirror provides a fascinating look at the roots of a book that galvanized America, and offers reflections on why, after all these years, this work retains its astonishing impact.<\/span>\n Równie ważne stają się spotkania, w trakcie których autorka rozmawia o tym, co czują rodzice, którzy decydują się na ten typ rodzicielstwa, kobiety, które przechodzą proces zapłodnienia in vitro, wreszcie dzieci, które nie znają tożsamości biologicznych rodziców.\n\nKsiążka Karoliny Domagalskiej to niezwykle cenny głos w dyskusji o współczesnych modelach rodziny. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18156352029890417027">John Howard Griffin was a white American journalist who is best known for his account, <i>Black Like Me</i>, in which he details the experience of darkening his skin and traveling as a black man through through Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama, and Georgia in 1959. (The racism that he encountered was so disturbing that he cut short the time that he had allotted for this very unique experiment, clearly dem</span>
  <span id="freeText18156352029890417027" style="display:none">John Howard Griffin was a white American journalist who is best known for his account, <i>Black Like Me</i>, in which he details the experience of darkening his skin and traveling as a black man through through Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama, and Georgia in 1959. (The racism that he encountered was so disturbing that he cut short the time that he had allotted for this very unique experiment, clearly demonstrating that no one would tolerate being treated as many blacks are, if he or she could possibly avoid it.)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18156352029890417027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
